# Title: Near-Surface Geophysical Imaging Textbook":

## Chapter: - Chapter 17: Seismic Migration:




### Section: 17.1 Pre-stack Migration:

Pre-stack migration is a powerful technique used in seismic imaging to improve the quality of seismic data. It is a process that involves the migration of seismic data from the subsurface to the surface, allowing for a more accurate interpretation of the subsurface structure. In this section, we will discuss the basics of pre-stack migration, including its definition, principles, and applications.

#### 17.1a Kirchhoff Migration

Kirchhoff migration is a type of pre-stack migration that is commonly used in the oil and gas industry. It is based on the Kirchhoff's integral theorem, which states that the displacement of a point on a surface is equal to the sum of the displacements of all points on the surface due to the forces acting on it. This theorem is used to derive the equations of equilibrium for a plate, which are essential for understanding the behavior of seismic waves in the subsurface.

The Kirchhoff migration process involves the use of the Fpplvon Krmn equations, which describe the equilibrium of a plate. These equations are derived from the weak form of the Kirchhoff plate, which is given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - 2M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_3 \delta w \,d\Omega\\
\end{align*}
$$

These equations describe the equilibrium of a plate under the action of external forces. The resulting governing equations are:

$$
\begin{align*}
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - 2M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_3 \delta w \,d\Omega\\
\end{align*}
$$

These equations are used to derive the Kirchhoff migration equations, which are essential for understanding the behavior of seismic waves in the subsurface. The Kirchhoff migration process involves the use of these equations to migrate seismic data from the subsurface to the surface, allowing for a more accurate interpretation of the subsurface structure.

### Subsection: 17.1b Post-stack Migration

Post-stack migration is another type of migration technique used in seismic imaging. It is commonly used in the oil and gas industry to improve the quality of seismic data. Unlike pre-stack migration, post-stack migration is applied to the stacked seismic data, which is the result of summing multiple traces of data.

The post-stack migration process involves the use of the Fpplvon Krmn equations, similar to pre-stack migration. However, in post-stack migration, the equations are applied to the stacked data, which has been summed from multiple traces. This allows for a more accurate migration of the data, as it takes into account the interactions between different traces.

The post-stack migration process involves the use of the Kirchhoff migration equations, which are derived from the Fpplvon Krmn equations. These equations are used to migrate the stacked data from the subsurface to the surface, allowing for a more accurate interpretation of the subsurface structure.

### Subsection: 17.1c Migration Velocity Analysis

Migration velocity analysis is a crucial step in the migration process. It involves the determination of the velocity of seismic waves in the subsurface, which is essential for accurately migrating the data. The velocity of seismic waves is affected by various factors, such as the properties of the subsurface material and the presence of interfaces between different layers.

The migration velocity analysis process involves the use of various techniques, such as the Kirchhoff migration equations and the Fpplvon Krmn equations. These equations are used to determine the velocity of seismic waves in the subsurface, which is then used to migrate the data accurately.

In conclusion, pre-stack and post-stack migration are powerful techniques used in seismic imaging to improve the quality of seismic data. They involve the use of various equations, such as the Kirchhoff migration equations and the Fpplvon Krmn equations, to migrate the data from the subsurface to the surface. Migration velocity analysis is a crucial step in the migration process, as it allows for accurate migration of the data. 


## Chapter 1:7: Seismic Migration:




#### 17.1b Wave-equation Migration

Wave-equation migration is another type of pre-stack migration that is commonly used in the oil and gas industry. It is based on the wave equation, which describes the propagation of seismic waves in the subsurface. The wave equation is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $u$ is the displacement of the seismic wave, $t$ is time, and $c$ is the wave velocity.

The wave-equation migration process involves the use of the wave equation to calculate the displacement of the seismic wave at any point in the subsurface. This displacement is then used to create an image of the subsurface structure.

One of the key advantages of wave-equation migration is that it can provide a more accurate image of the subsurface structure compared to Kirchhoff migration. This is because wave-equation migration takes into account the effects of wave propagation, while Kirchhoff migration does not.

However, wave-equation migration is also more computationally intensive and requires more advanced mathematical techniques compared to Kirchhoff migration. Therefore, it is often used in conjunction with Kirchhoff migration to provide a more comprehensive understanding of the subsurface structure.

In the next section, we will discuss the applications of pre-stack migration in the oil and gas industry.

#### 17.1c Applications of Pre-stack Migration

Pre-stack migration has a wide range of applications in the oil and gas industry. It is used to improve the quality of seismic data, enhance the interpretation of subsurface structures, and aid in the identification of hydrocarbon reservoirs. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of pre-stack migration.

##### Improving Seismic Data Quality

One of the primary applications of pre-stack migration is to improve the quality of seismic data. Pre-stack migration techniques, such as Kirchhoff migration and wave-equation migration, can be used to reduce noise and improve the resolution of seismic images. This is particularly important in areas with complex geology, where the propagation of seismic waves can be affected by multiple layers and interfaces.

##### Enhancing Subsurface Structure Interpretation

Pre-stack migration can also be used to enhance the interpretation of subsurface structures. By providing a more accurate image of the subsurface, pre-stack migration can help geologists and engineers to better understand the geometry and properties of subsurface structures. This can aid in the identification of potential hydrocarbon reservoirs and the planning of drilling operations.

##### Aiding in Hydrocarbon Reservoir Identification

The identification of hydrocarbon reservoirs is a key application of pre-stack migration. By improving the quality of seismic data and enhancing the interpretation of subsurface structures, pre-stack migration can aid in the identification of potential hydrocarbon reservoirs. This is particularly important in areas where the presence of hydrocarbons is not obvious from the surface.

In conclusion, pre-stack migration is a powerful tool in the oil and gas industry, with a wide range of applications. By improving the quality of seismic data, enhancing the interpretation of subsurface structures, and aiding in the identification of hydrocarbon reservoirs, pre-stack migration plays a crucial role in the exploration and development of hydrocarbon resources.




#### 17.1c Reverse Time Migration

Reverse time migration (RTM) is a powerful pre-stack migration technique that has gained popularity in recent years due to its ability to provide high-quality images of the subsurface structure. Unlike traditional pre-stack migration techniques, such as Kirchhoff migration and wave-equation migration, which are based on the wave equation, RTM is based on the reverse time principle.

The reverse time principle states that the propagation of seismic waves in the subsurface can be modeled by reversing the time direction. This means that the seismic waves travel backwards in time, from the receiver to the source. This principle is based on the assumption that the subsurface structure is time-invariant, meaning that it does not change over time.

The RTM process involves the use of the reverse time principle to calculate the displacement of the seismic wave at any point in the subsurface. This displacement is then used to create an image of the subsurface structure. The RTM process can be represented mathematically as:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $u$ is the displacement of the seismic wave, $t$ is time, and $c$ is the wave velocity.

One of the key advantages of RTM is that it can provide a more accurate image of the subsurface structure compared to traditional pre-stack migration techniques. This is because RTM takes into account the effects of wave propagation, while traditional techniques do not. Additionally, RTM can also provide a more detailed image of the subsurface structure, as it can resolve smaller features and structures.

However, RTM also has some limitations. It requires a high-quality seismic dataset and can be computationally intensive. Additionally, the reverse time principle may not always hold true in all subsurface structures, leading to errors in the migration process.

Despite its limitations, RTM has proven to be a valuable tool in the oil and gas industry, particularly in the exploration and development of hydrocarbon reservoirs. Its ability to provide high-quality images of the subsurface structure has made it an essential technique for geophysicists and engineers. As technology continues to advance, it is likely that RTM will become even more widely used in the industry.





#### 17.1d Least-squares Migration

Least-squares migration (LSM) is another powerful pre-stack migration technique that has gained popularity in recent years. Unlike RTM, which is based on the reverse time principle, LSM is based on the least-squares method.

The least-squares method is a mathematical technique used to approximate the solution of a system of linear equations. In the context of geophysical imaging, LSM is used to approximate the solution of the wave equation.

The LSM process involves the use of the least-squares method to calculate the displacement of the seismic wave at any point in the subsurface. This displacement is then used to create an image of the subsurface structure. The LSM process can be represented mathematically as:

$$
\min_{u} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial x^2} - c^2 \right)^2
$$

where $u$ is the displacement of the seismic wave, $t$ is time, and $c$ is the wave velocity.

One of the key advantages of LSM is that it can provide a more accurate image of the subsurface structure compared to traditional pre-stack migration techniques. This is because LSM takes into account the effects of wave propagation, while traditional techniques do not. Additionally, LSM can also provide a more detailed image of the subsurface structure, as it can resolve smaller features and structures.

However, LSM also has some limitations. It requires a high-quality seismic dataset and can be computationally intensive. Additionally, the least-squares method may not always provide the most accurate solution, especially for complex subsurface structures.

Despite its limitations, LSM has proven to be a valuable tool in the oil and gas industry, particularly in areas with complex subsurface structures. Its ability to provide high-quality images of the subsurface structure makes it an essential technique for geophysical imaging.

## Chapter 1:7: Seismic Migration:

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of seismic migration, a powerful technique used in geophysical imaging to enhance the quality of seismic data. We have learned that seismic migration is a process that involves the movement of seismic energy from its original location to a new location, resulting in a more accurate representation of the subsurface. This technique is particularly useful in near-surface imaging, where the complexities of the Earth's surface can significantly affect the quality of seismic data.

We have also discussed the different types of seismic migration, including time migration and depth migration. Time migration is a technique that involves the movement of seismic energy in time, while depth migration involves the movement of seismic energy in depth. Each of these techniques has its own advantages and is used in different scenarios.

Furthermore, we have delved into the mathematical principles behind seismic migration. We have learned that seismic migration is based on the concept of wave propagation, where seismic energy travels through the Earth's subsurface. We have also explored the mathematical equations that govern the process of seismic migration, including the wave equation and the migration equation.

In conclusion, seismic migration is a crucial tool in near-surface geophysical imaging. It allows us to obtain a more accurate representation of the subsurface, which is essential for various applications such as oil and gas exploration, groundwater mapping, and structural geology studies. By understanding the principles and techniques of seismic migration, we can enhance the quality of our seismic data and make more informed decisions about the Earth's subsurface.

# Title: Near-Surface Geophysical Imaging Textbook":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Seismic AVO Analysis:




### Section: 18.1 AVO Theory:

The Amplitude Variation with Offset (AVO) analysis is a powerful tool in geophysics that allows us to study the properties of the Earth's subsurface. It is based on the Zoeppritz equations, which describe the reflection and transmission of seismic waves at the interface between two different media. These equations are essential for understanding the behavior of seismic waves and their interaction with the Earth's subsurface.

#### 18.1a Zoeppritz Equations

The Zoeppritz equations consist of four equations with four unknowns: the reflected P-wave amplitude coefficient (R<sub>P</sub>), the reflected S-wave amplitude coefficient (R<sub>S</sub>), the transmitted P-wave amplitude coefficient (T<sub>P</sub>), and the transmitted S-wave amplitude coefficient (T<sub>S</sub>). These equations are given by:

$$
R_P = \frac{V_P^2 - V_S^2}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \cos^2(\theta_1) + \frac{2V_PV_S}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \cos(\theta_1)\cos(\theta_2)\cos(\phi_1)\cos(\phi_2)
$$

$$
R_S = \frac{V_P^2 - V_S^2}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \sin^2(\theta_1) + \frac{2V_PV_S}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \cos(\theta_1)\cos(\theta_2)\sin(\phi_1)\sin(\phi_2)
$$

$$
T_P = \frac{2V_PV_S}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \cos(\theta_1)\cos(\theta_2)\cos(\phi_1)\cos(\phi_2)
$$

$$
T_S = \frac{2V_PV_S}{V_P^2 + V_S^2} \cos(\theta_1)\cos(\theta_2)\sin(\phi_1)\sin(\phi_2)
$$

where <math>{\theta}_{1}</math>=angle of incidence, <math>{\theta}_{2}</math>=angle of the transmitted P-wave, <math>{\phi}_{1}</math>=angle of reflected S-wave and <math>{\phi}_{2}</math>=angle of the transmitted S-wave.

These equations can be solved for the four unknowns, but they do not provide an intuitive understanding of how the reflection amplitudes vary with the rock properties involved (density, velocity, etc.). Several attempts have been made to develop approximations to the Zoeppritz equations, such as Bortfeld's (1961) and Aki & Richards (1980), but the most successful of these is the Shuey's approximation.

#### 18.1b Shuey Equation

The Shuey equation is a simplified form of the Zoeppritz equations that provides a more intuitive understanding of how the reflection amplitudes vary with the rock properties involved. It is given by:

$$
R(0) + G\Delta\theta + F\Delta\theta^2 = 0
$$

where <math>R(0)</math> is the reflection coefficient at normal incidence and is controlled by the contrast in acoustic impedances, <math>G</math> is often referred to as the AVO gradient, and <math>F</math> describes the behavior at large angles/far offsets that are close to the critical angle.

The Shuey equation can be further simplified by assuming that the angle of incidence is less than 30 degrees (i.e., the offset is relatively small), so the third term will be truncated. This simplification is often used in practice and provides a good approximation of the Zoeppritz equations.

In the next section, we will discuss how the Shuey equation can be used to interpret AVO data and understand the properties of the Earth's subsurface.
